              role of individual genes in disease-relevant mechanisms. However,
              modern models of the architecture of complex traits predict that
              gene-gene interactions play a crucial role in disease origin and
              progression. PhenoPLIER's representation is based on modules of
              genes with similar expression patterns across the same conditions.
              We observe that diseases are significantly associated with gene
              modules expressed in relevant cell types, and our approach is
              accurate in predicting known drug-disease pairs and inferring
              mechanisms of action. Furthermore, using a CRISPR screen to
              analyze lipid regulation, we find that functionally important
              players lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated
              modules by PhenoPLIER. In conclusion, by incorporating groups of
              co-expressed genes, PhenoPLIER can contextualize genetic
              associations and reveal potential targets missed by single-gene
              strategies. PhenoPLIER provides a novel framework for interpreting
              genetic associations through the lens of gene modules.

              genes collectively influence complex traits across different
              contexts. This mechanistic understanding of gene co-expression,
              pivotal for the development of new therapies, is often limited by
              the focus on single-gene strategies, thereby overlooking the
              potential targets that may be revealed through the interaction of
              gene modules. To address this gap, we have developed PhenoPLIER, a
              novel framework that incorporates groups of co-expressed genes to
              contextualize genetic associations. Our study demonstrates that
              diseases are significantly associated with gene modules expressed
              in relevant cell types. PhenoPLIER accurately predicts known
              drug-disease pairs and infers mechanisms of action. Furthermore,
              our CRISPR screen analysis of lipid regulation revealed that while
              functionally important players may lack associations, they are
              prioritized in trait-associated modules by PhenoPLIER. In
              conclusion, PhenoPLIER not only provides a more comprehensive
              interpretation of genetic associations but also highlights
              potential therapeutic targets overlooked by single-gene
              strategies. This advancement in the understanding of gene
              co-expression and its role in disease etiology and drug mechanisms
              could potentially revolutionize the field of therapeutic targets
              and drug repurposing.

              In the realm of genetic studies and functional genomics,
              understanding the co-expression of genes under varying conditions
              is essential for discerning their influence on complex traits and

              effectively map these interactions and their pharmacological
              implications. To address this, we present PhenoPLIER, a novel

              expression patterns across identical conditions. Our results

              inferring mechanisms of action. An additional application of our

              functionally important players that were previously overlooked in

              conclusion, PhenoPLIER fills a critical gap in the field,
              enhancing our understanding of disease etiology, drug mechanisms,

              repurposing and improved clustering of complex traits.

              Genes operate symbiotically within specific contexts to execute
              their functions. A comprehensive understanding of gene expression
              regulation across various conditions is essential for discerning
              how these genes affect complex traits. Such insight is pivotal for
              the creation of new therapies. Transcriptome-wide association

              contemporary models of complex trait architecture suggest that
              interactions between genes are vital to the onset and progression
              of diseases. In this paper, we present PhenoPLIER, a computational
              method that amalgamates gene-trait associations and
              pharmacological perturbation data into a single latent
              representation for joint analysis. This representation hinges on
              modules of genes exhibiting similar expression patterns under
              identical conditions. We found that diseases have a significant
              correlation with gene modules expressed in relevant cell types.
              Our method accurately predicts known drug-disease pairs and infers
              mechanisms of action. Moreover, a CRISPR screen used to examine
              lipid regulation revealed that functionally significant genes may
              lack associations but are prioritized in trait-associated modules
              by PhenoPLIER.

              Within the broader field of genomics, the interplay of genes and
              their collective influence on complex traits is a critical area of
              study, particularly for the development of new therapies. While
              transcriptome-wide association studies have shed light on the role
              of individual genes in disease mechanisms, the current literature
              lacks comprehensive models that account for gene-gene

              and progression. To fill this gap, we introduce PhenoPLIER, a
              novel computational approach that amalgamates gene-trait
              associations and pharmacological perturbation data into a unified
              latent representation for joint analysis. This innovative method
              hinges on the identification of gene modules, groups of genes
              exhibiting similar expression patterns under identical conditions.

              accurate in predicting known drug-disease pairs and deducing

              regulation indicates that while some functionally important genes
              lack direct associations, they are prioritized in trait-associated
              modules by PhenoPLIER. This conclusion not only answers the
              initial question of gene-gene interactions in disease progression
              but also advances the broader field by providing a novel approach
              to understanding complex trait architecture and disease
              mechanisms.
